### 5. Continuous Delivery

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is continuous delivery?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=1)** - There are three key stages in [[Software Development]].
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=4)** The build where you compile and test code and turn it into software, the deploy where you take and run that software and test it, and the release where you send that software to an end user or, more usually nowadays, you deploy it in a production environment where someone else can use it
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=20)** - In the old way of delivering software, the whole application isn't built and run until major milestones are completed.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=27)** Then there's a very large and complex integration build and a long test phase generating large batches of bug reports long after the code was first written.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=37)** It's error prone and wasteful.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=39)** - [Brown Shirt] So instead, what if you build, deploy, and release the application with every change a developer makes?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=46)** - [Red Shirt] [[Continuous Integration (CI)|Continuous integration]] is the practice of automatically building and [[Unit Testing]] the entire application frequently, ideally on every source code check-in so that the application is always in a working state.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=59)** - [Brown Shirt] [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|Continuous delivery]] is the additional practice of deploying every change to a production-like test environment and performing automated integration and acceptance testing so that the application is always release ready.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=73)** You'll hear people abbreviate the combination of both of these as CI/CD.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=78)** - [Red Shirt] And then the real pro move, continuous deployment is the practice of releasing the application automatically into its production environment after the testing says it's ready to go.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=90)** Large scale web properties, like Amazon, Meta, [[Google]], and Wells Fargo, use continuous deployment.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=97)** - Using these techniques decreases the time it takes to deploy a change and allows for rapid experimentation and market validation of ideas.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=106)** - [Red Shirt] This is following the first way of [[DevOps]], By optimizing end-to-end flow through the entire system and it lets you get a product to market faster.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=114)** - [Brown Shirt] Yeah, organizations using continuous deployment might deploy a given application 10 times or more daily.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=121)** But wait, isn't this reckless?
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=124)** You might think with rapid cycles and high frequency of change, you would see a decrease in quality.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=130)** - But research consistently shows that these teams have a lower change failure rate than their peers.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=136)** Moving faster means fewer bugs, not more.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=139)** - This increase in quality happens because instead of checking that everything works only at the end of the development cycle, we're testing earlier in the process.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=147)** Instead of one huge release consisting of hundreds of changes, we evaluate and deploy changes one by one, testing every commit and making sure the software is always in a running state.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=157)** - [Red Shirt] If you've been paying attention, you may be shouting "Second way" at your screen and alarming your coworkers, family or pets, and you'd be right.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=168)** CI leverages the ability to make fast feedback loops, which lets you find and fix issues quickly and drives up your quality while you also drive up your velocity.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=178)** And then you can safely get those changes all the way out to production as soon as possible.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=183)** - Lean teaches us that a high level of work in progress, abbreviated WIP, is dangerous.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=189)** Code that's sitting around in your system undelivered, it's generating risk and waste.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=195)** - At a large enterprise where we once worked, we performed these monthly releases to a large [[E-Commerce]] site that would start on a Friday evening and take eight hours, manually releasing 30 or so applications into an environment that had a hundred apps in it.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=211)** Nothing about that was good.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=213)** People had to wait for a monthly release so they'd panic and cut corners if they were going to miss it.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=219)** The release itself was painful and error-prone, and when inevitably something went wrong, you had 30 developers pointing fingers at each other sure that someone else was causing the site to go down.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=232)** - The State of DevOps Report found that high performers reported that their lead time required to deploy changes into production was less than one hour, whereas low performers required lead times between one to six months.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=244)** - That's a huge competitive advantage, but the benefits extend past the development cycle.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=250)** There are two advantages to a continuous delivery environment that make your mean time to recover from production problems shorter too.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=258)** - The first benefit is that using continuous delivery makes it easier to find the source of failures because, hey, you're only rolling out one change at a time.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=267)** - Then when you do find a problem, a bug fix can be treated just like any other change and rolled out quickly via continuous integration and delivery without breaking your process.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=278)** - Okay, for example, I once had an app that exhibited a slow leak of database connections over time.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=284)** It grew and grew over a few days until it hit its limit and everything broke.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=289)** This type of slow leak over time problem can sometimes consume a lot of resources trying to find out why this is happening.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=296)** But since we practiced all the things that we've talked about here, like small and frequent deploys, we had an advantage.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=303)** By overlaying our database connection growth graph with the deploys that happened that week, we could quickly figure out precisely which production deployment correlated with the increase of database connections.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=314)** Once we had the deployment ID, we could determine which exact code commit introduced the leak and we rolled out a fix within hours using our normal CI/CD system.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=323)** - [Red Shirt] That's cool.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=325)** Next, we'll talk about how to implement continuous integration in a build pipeline.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Six practices for continuous integration](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=0)** - We've defined [[Continuous Integration (CI)|continuous integration]], [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]], and continuous deployment.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=5)** But how do they fit together in practice?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=9)** Think of them as a pipeline with one stage flowing into the next from build to deploy to release.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=15)** Each of them depends on the previous step being performed correctly.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=19)** First, I'll talk about keeping our software in a working state all of the time by using continuous integration in our build stage.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=26)** In a continuous integration build system, on each commit, there's an automatically triggered build that takes the entire code base, builds it, runs all the unit tests and other code validation steps and packages an artifact at the end of the build, along with a build status and a log.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=42)** There are many open source and commercial CI systems, but they're all similar at their core.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=47)** If any step fails, the build is broken and the ability to move past that step is blocked for the entire team.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=53)** Fixing the build requires a new change that runs through the exact same process.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=57)** This discipline generates a fast feedback loop for code flaws and it helps reduce rework.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=62)** Now it sounds simple, but there's a discipline required to make it work effectively.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=67)** Here are six practices that will help you succeed at continuous integration.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=72)** The first practice: builds should run quickly.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=76)** People often ask, does the build pass the coffee test?
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=80)** Well, the build should take less time than it takes to go get a cup of coffee.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=84)** Now, I'm not a professional barista, but for practical purposes, let's call that five minutes.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=90)** The longer a build takes, the more people will naturally wait until they have a larger batch of changes to run the build, which increases your work in progress or WIP.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=98)** High WIP leads to lots of problems in the system, which brings us to the second practice.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=105)** Commit small changes.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=107)** Seek to commit the smallest amount of code per commit.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=111)** Small changes are much easier for everyone on the team to reason about, and it also makes isolating the failure way easier.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=118)** Oh yeah, failures. That brings us to practice three.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=121)** Don't leave the build broken. The build is going to break.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=125)** That's not a problem. It's the system working as designed.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=128)** But when you leave the build broken, you block delivery.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=132)** I often suggest that the team make a pact to delay meetings or stop all of their work until the build is fixed.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=138)** This is a cultural problem and how you handle broken build sets the tone for your delivery culture.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=143)** Okay, speaking of culture, practice number four, use a trunk based development flow.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=149)** The state of [[DevOps]] report identifies this practice as a hallmark of high performing teams.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=155)** There are two main practices that people use when developing; branch-based and trunk based.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=160)** In branch-based development, each developer makes their own copy or branch of the source code, works on it for a long time while they incorporate entire new features and then try to merge back into the main line or trunk.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=173)** The longer and larger the branch, the more problematic that is.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=177)** Trunk based means that there are no long running branches and developers make small changes and check them back into trunk multiple times a day.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=186)** As a result, the trunk is always up to date across all developers.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=191)** But what about major features that you don't want to release until they're ready?
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=195)** Instead of keeping them in long running code branches, developers can branch in code and use feature flags to define whether those features are user visible or not.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=204)** Now, having worked in both styles, I highly recommend using a trunk based approach.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=210)** It helps keep work in progress down to a minimal amount.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=213)** It ensures that code is reviewed and checked frequently and reduces wasteful and error prone work to merge branches.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=220)** Okay, practice number five.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=222)** Don't allow flaky automated tests. Fix them.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=226)** Have you ever worked somewhere where the tests sometimes failed and sometimes passed for no real apparent reason?
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=231)** Well, I have and it was not a ton of fun.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=235)** You have no way to know if you can really trust the CI system and if your build artifact was any good.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=241)** Oh, speaking of artifacts, this brings us to practice six.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=245)** The build should return a status, a log, and an artifact.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=248)** The status should be a simple pass fail or red green that tells you whether the build is good to move along in the pipeline.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=255)** A build log is a detailed record of all the tests run and results of the run, which helps with troubleshooting and compliance.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=263)** The artifact is an installable version of the application.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=266)** It should be uploaded and tagged with the build number.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=268)** This adds trust and assures auditability and immutability of the artifacts.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=273)** These six practices will help you jumpstart your continuous integration efforts.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/six-practices-for-continuous-integration?u=76281980&t=278)** Take a moment and use the course notebook to reflect and write down the next steps you could take to implement some of these six practices in a build pipeline you work with.

#### [Five practices for continuous delivery](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=15)** The definitive work on continuous delivery is the excellent book of the same name by Jez Humble and Dave Farley, and we highly recommend it.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=24)** A great quote from the book to think about is, "It's not how much you can deliver, but how little."
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=30)** - [Instructor] After you have meditated upon this truth, let's return to our pipeline.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=35)** After the build stage, we have a deployment stage.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=38)** It's first step is to deploy our latest successful build artifacts to a live environment that's as much of a copy of production as possible.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=47)** You might call this environment CI, staging, test, or pre-production.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=52)** At this point, the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of your testing should happen and it should be automated as much as possible.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=58)** - Let's talk about five techniques to be successful at continuous delivery.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=62)** In the video on [[Continuous Integration (CI)|continuous integration]], we discussed creating an artifact upon successful completion of the build.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=69)** That artifact is whatever your technology stack uses, RPM or Debian package, MSI installers, [[Java]] WAR files, ZIP files.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=78)** It's a version bundled of all your technical pieces of your component.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=82)** - These artifacts shouldn't be rebuilt for staging, testing, and production environments.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=87)** - Yeah, that's right.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=88)** They get built once and then used in all environments.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=91)** This way, you know all your testing steps are valid since they are all used by the same artifact.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=97)** - [Instructor] Your artifacts also shouldn't be allowed to change along the way.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=101)** They need to be stored and have permission set in such a way so that they're immutable.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=106)** - In the continuous delivery pipelines I've built, I set the permission so that only the CI system can write the artifact to the artifact repository, and the deployment system only has read access to that artifact.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=118)** - We want artifacts to be immutable for two reasons.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=121)** - Yeah, first, it's going to create trust between the teams when they're debugging an issue.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=126)** You want dev and ops and QA, you want them to all have confidence that the underlying bits didn't change underneath them between stages.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=133)** - Yeah, a quick checksum can prove that you're all looking at the exact same artifact version.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=139)** - The second is auditability.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=142)** One of the great parts about building a continuous delivery pipeline is that you can trace specific code versions in source control to a successful build artifact to a running system.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=152)** - Rebuilding or changing an artifact along the way would break your auditability.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=158)** Next, you want to have a pre-production environment that's as identical as possible to your production environment.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=165)** - This environment needs to include all the load balancers, network settings, and security controls, along with all the data that matches production.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=174)** - One of the reasons we move code to this environment is to do all the acceptance testing, smoke tests, and integration tests that are difficult to fully simulate on dev desktops or build servers.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=186)** - Yeah, this gives you confidence that both your code and your deployment process is going to work correctly in production.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=193)** - This brings up another crucial point.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=196)** Your system needs to stop the pipeline if there's breakage at any point.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=200)** - Yeah, if any stage fails, it should stop the next build phase from happening.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=204)** If a build is broken, don't deploy.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=207)** If a deploy is broken, don't release.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=209)** Stop and fix it.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=211)** - People will complain and say that, "Well, but I'm blocked from moving forward with my work until the build's fixed."
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=219)** But that's the point.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=220)** You want to optimize for overall flow of your whole software delivery, not for each individual person.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=226)** Tell them to pitch in and fix the problem.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=230)** We talked about this in our "[[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as Code]]" chapter, but it's good to reiterate that item potency is key for our deployments.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=238)** That is, no matter how many times you run the same deployment, you get the exact same resulting system.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=245)** - You may accomplish that using an immutable packaging mechanism like Docker containers or through using a [[Configuration Management]] tool like Puppet or Chef.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=252)** But however you do it, you can't have variability in your pipeline or else you just can't trust it.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/five-practices-for-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=259)** - There are other things you have to consider to get good at continuous delivery, but these should get you started.

>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=3)** Faster deploys, fewer bugs, less whip, less technical debt, developers and operations living together.

>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=61)** - We'll start with the most developer-centric testing methodologies and gradually work our way to testing that occurs near the end of our pipelines.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=69)** - [Instructor] Or, if you're familiar with Mike Cohn's test pyramid, we'll be working from the bottom up, starting with the fastest, more code focused testing and moving to slower, full [[End-to-end Testing]].
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=81)** First is [[Unit Testing]].
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=83)** These are tests written by the developer as part of the code to validate that individual functions work correctly.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=90)** Let's say you have a calculator application with a function called add that takes two numbers and adds 'em together.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=98)** - In unit testing, we would write a test inside the code base to validate that function.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=103)** This is usually the fastest testing available.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=107)** You write code to skip over or stub out external dependencies.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=110)** Unit tests are run by the developer, on their own machine, as they write code.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=116)** - Next up, code hygiene.

>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=121)** - Yes, but you should actually do it.

> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=124)** - Okay, yeah.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=125)** Code hygiene testing checks your code for best practices for your particular language or framework.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=130)** It's usually accomplished through tools called linters or formatters, and you got to keep up the code hygiene.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=137)** You don't want to end up at the [[DevOps]] dentist.

>

>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=144)** Onto [[Integration Testing]].
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=146)** It's performed with all the apps, components and dependencies operating in a test environment, and tests are written to make sure the components work well, both individually and together.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=156)** - And then you have acceptance or end-to-end testing, often done at a UI level, that tests the entire end product as an end user would experience it.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=167)** All of these can and should be automated.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=170)** A manual check before you unleash a new feature on your users is still fine, but let the computers do the thousands of combinations you need to do good testing.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=179)** - That's a lot of kinds of testing to automate.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=182)** How do we set up a culture and process that encourages us to actually write them?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=187)** This is where a test driven development and behavior driven development comes in.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=192)** - [Instructor] TDD and BDD are development practices that start with writing tests or checking for desired behaviors before you write the code that it tests.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=202)** - [James] Yeah.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=203)** With this approach, the flow of our calculator example would be that you write the desired output of the ad function and then that test starts failing because there's nothing there to make it work yet.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=213)** Then you write the function and once you get it right, the test starts passing.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=218)** - Now you're creating a comprehensive test suite while the application is being developed.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=224)** - Okay, so far all the testing we've talked about has been reasonably fast, so you can put it into your build pipeline and still have good build times.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=232)** But what about slower types of tests that have to run for a long time, like browser compatibility test or large compliance test suites?
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=241)** - Slow tests can't be ignored if there's no practical way to speed them up.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=246)** - Yeah.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=247)** To get around this, there's three strategies to use.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=249)** Run slow tests in parallel in your pipeline, not blocking until the end release.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=255)** Use scheduled testing like a nightly test suite and run slow test suites continuously against your test environment.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=263)** It can be okay to release a non-critical bug and then find it overnight.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=267)** In a CD world, you can fix it very quickly.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=270)** Does every release need to wait on that test to make sure it works perfectly in super old browsers?
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=276)** Balance the cost of delay versus the cost of bugs.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=280)** - There are many other specialty tests you'll need.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=283)** Infrastructure testing, performance testing, and [[Security Testing]] to name a few.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=288)** - Yeah, testing is a huge domain, but getting good at automated testing is your single most significant factor in successful continuous delivery.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/the-role-of-qa-in-devops?u=76281980&t=297)** If you embrace it and stick with it, you'll be moving faster than those that don't.

#### [Continuous deployment: The final frontier](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=0)** - Now that you're doing [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|Continuous Delivery]], what about Continuous Deployment?
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=4)** Push that change all the way live as soon as it passes testing?
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=8)** - Now, some organizations don't feel comfortable with it, and that's okay.

>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=52)** Once it passes all your tests, the artifact is released.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=56)** This means market it in some way, as having been released to production at a given time.

>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=70)** So is this just Continuous Delivery done a second time?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=75)** - Well, yes and no.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=76)** No, because production releases often require significant engineering work to safely automate.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=82)** If you're releasing packaged software, you only have to worry about data and configuration working right with a new version.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=89)** But if you release a running service, you have live users and flowing data to contend with.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=95)** But also, yes, because whatever complex release procedure you create, you have to go back and implement that same thing in your test environment, or else you've introduced a unique and not continually tested part of your system.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=108)** If you do that, it's bad and you should feel bad.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=112)** - Wow, Ernest, you're being rather opinionated today.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=116)** But maybe that's because when you implement Continuous Deployment, you need it to be an opinionated system.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=122)** In other words, there should be one right way to do it that the system makes very easy.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=128)** - There are many different Production Release Patterns you can use depending on your specific needs.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=133)** In general, you want them to be safe, reversible, and to provide no downtime to active users.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=140)** - [Host] Some patterns you might see are a Rolling Deployment.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=144)** In other words, upgrading one of many identical systems at a time, to allow seamless shifting of traffic.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=151)** - [Ernest] A Blue-Green Deployment, where you create an entire new version of the system and then cut user traffic over from the current or Blue System to the new or Green System.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=162)** This could be swapping persistent test and production environments or making a new one from scratch in the Cloud.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=170)** - [Host] A Canary Deployment, where you only upgrade one of many identical systems and let it run for a while, to see if it exhibits any problems under production load.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=181)** - [Ernest] An A/B Deployment, where you release feature flagged software, and then use a system to open up the new features only to a certain subset of users, either briefly as a Canary Test, or longer for a Public Beta Test.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=195)** - [Host] How you can perform a Production Deployment is very dependent on your packaging choice, [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|infrastructure as code]] strategy, and the [[Software Architecture]] of your service.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=205)** - You have to design your deployment in collaboration with the people designing those other parts.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=210)** - You're right, Ernest.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=211)** I built out Continuous Deployment for Signal Sciences, a security startup that started with a small team of five, but quickly grew to over 100.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=221)** At that time, there wasn't much tooling out there, so we wrote an internal tool called Deployer, based off of Etsy's similar Deployinator Tool.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=229)** Once we had [[Continuous Integration (CI)|Continuous Integration]] and Continuous Delivery in place, anyone in the company could go to Deployer and deploy the latest build to staging, and if it passed automated testing and it did what the developer expected, then it was eligible to deploy to production.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=245)** Now, it didn't happen automatically, but it literally was a push of a button.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=249)** We followed one of the mantras we talked about earlier.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=252)** From code commit to running in production, it took five minutes or less.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=256)** We used a Rolling Deployment Pattern, along with the A/B Deployment Pattern, using feature flags.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=261)** Developers would often release features to internal users, early adopters, and then all the customers over a few weeks to make sure the usage was what we expected and that the new features were delightful to customers.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=273)** Funny enough, Deployer even had a mode where it would deploy itself.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=278)** This made our entire pipeline something that any engineer could make changes to and deploy, via self-service automation.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/continuous-deployment-the-final-frontier?u=76281980&t=285)** - That's awesome, deployment is the first place the DEV and OPs worlds collide, whether they want to or not, and can make for a great end [[User Experience (UX)|user experience]] or a terrible one.

#### [Your DevOps CI toolchain](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=0)** - When selecting tools for CI toolchains, it's tempting to start from the developer and work your way out to the delivered service or software.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=9)** - In fact, people often refer to a [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] toolchain as a pipeline, because code flows in one end and finished product out the other.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=18)** That makes sense, but for your continuous delivery toolchain, we're going to encourage you to approach it a little differently,
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=25)** - And it takes different strokes to move the world.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=28)** - [James] Instead of a left-to-right flow, think of these as layers to an onion.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=32)** We're going to start at the outer layer and work our way in, because we want to consider the entire toolchain from the perspective of what we want the end state to be.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=40)** - The outermost layer of the onion is deployment.
>

>

> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=72)** - If you decided on rolling deploys, you'll need orchestration for rolling out releases across your fleet, and this will depend on your infrastructure tooling.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=80)** For example, if you're using [[Kubernetes]] or Serverless, they'll have options built into their platform.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=86)** Otherwise, you might use deployment orchestration built in [[Ansible]] or Salt.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=92)** These decisions affect your runtime system, and therefore cascade all the way back to the front end of your pipeline, driving tool choices at those other stages.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=101)** - Okay, now if we move one layer in, we have the artifact repository.

>

>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=265)** This is what actually performs the build work and initiates the later stages in the pipeline.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=271)** - [[Jenkins]] is open source, and is popular in many organizations.

> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=284)** - [Instructor] Or you might be inclined to use [[SaaS]]-based offerings for CI, like CloudBees and CircleCI.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=290)** - GitHub even offers build pipelines built into their [[Version Control]] system nowadays, called GitHub Actions.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=296)** It's popular for its simplicity.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=299)** - And that leads us to the very middle of the onion, version control.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=303)** - Ah, don't make me cry.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=304)** - [Instructor] (sighs) Version control is where we commit code changes and view the entire history of all changes ever made.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=312)** It allows the developers to stay in sync with each other by treating each change as an independent layer.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=318)** - [James] Most organizations today opt to use [[Git]] in the form of GitHub, GitLab, or [[Bitbucket]], either as a SaaS or as an on-prem enterprise version.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=329)** - But there are other specialized version control systems, like Perforce and PlasticSCM if you need to manage very large binary assets in source control.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=340)** Once you've planned out your CD pipeline, trace a single code change through it and measure how fast it moves from a developer system to production.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=350)** This is your overall cycle time.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=353)** - That's right.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=353)** Record it, log it, graph it, and actively share this number with the team.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=360)** Over time, it will get worse if you don't actively work to make it better.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=365)** What better looks like for your team depends on your toolchain, but try to make choices that lower overall cycle time, not increase it.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=374)** - And that's your CI toolchain in a nutshell.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=377)** - Or in an onion skin.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=379)** (instructor sighs)
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-ci-toolchain?u=76281980&t=380)** - Links to all the tools we've mentioned and more are in the course handout in the example files.

### 6. Site Reliability Engineering

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is site reliability engineering?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=0)** - The fifth and last practice area of [[DevOps]] is site reliability engineering.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=5)** - Engineering is the application of theoretical principles to solve real world problems, and in engineering, reliability describes the ability of a system to perform its intended function correctly and consistently when it's expected to.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=20)** - And in IT this includes availability, performance, security, and all the other factors that allow your service to actually deliver its capability to users.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=31)** - SRE isn't just for websites, but the term got coined at [[Google]] for engineers focused on the reliability of their website and the term stuck.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=40)** - Yeah, Google Engineers published a book called Site Reliability Engineering that's also available online for free that you can use to learn more about their perspective on it.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=48)** We've actually linked to it in the course handout.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=51)** - [Instructor 1] You can think of SRE as the actual operations part of DevOps.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=56)** [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as code]] lets us use software to define our system.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=60)** [[Continuous Integration (CI)|Continuous integration]] lets us build and deploy our system and our applications, but then once you have a production service, you need to monitor it, manage it, and fix things when they go wrong.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=71)** - [Instructor 2] Yeah, many people define site reliability engineering as a discipline of using a software engineering approach to automate all these operational processes,
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=81)** - [Instructor 1] But that's an incomplete view.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=83)** To create effective reliability, you need to engineer your systems and applications for reliability from the start.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=90)** You can't just bolt on reliability once something goes live.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=93)** - [Instructor 2] Yeah, Patrick Debois identified four key areas of DevOps.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=98)** Extending delivery to production, extending feedback from operations to dev, embedding dev into operations, and embedding ops into dev.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=110)** - We're going to use this to illustrate a holistic approach to reliability engineering, but we're going to simplify it into building reliability and operational feedback.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=120)** - [Instructor 2] In building for reliability, we examine how you construct your system to be maximally resilient and maintainable in the first place.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=128)** - And then in operational feedback, we'll talk about observability and [[Incident Response]] practices and how to feed back that information from production operations to improve the service.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=139)** When you bring together both practices, you have the DevOps take on site reliability engineering.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=145)** - Yeah, and a solid SRE practice will improve key metrics that have been shown to drive business outcomes.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=151)** - It'll improve your change failure rate, which is how many production changes cause issues, through reliability testing and deployment automation.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=160)** - It will also improve your time to restore service, which is how long it takes to fix a production problem when it happens through good problem detection and operational automation and discipline.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=171)** - And it'll improve your ability to meet uptime and performance goals through observability and resilience.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/what-is-site-reliability-engineering?u=76281980&t=177)** - Okay. Let's get to it.

#### [Building for reliability: Theory](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=0)** - When you start talking about operations, people immediately envision the activities that happen in production.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=7)** But most of your success in production is based on decisions you make at design time and your [[Software Architecture]].
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=14)** Developers, let's talk about your role in creating apps that can be reliable.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=20)** That's a huge topic, so I'll hit three key resources that address how you design your applications for a real systems environment to make your services work great, even in adverse conditions.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=32)** Software engineering has developed standard [[Design Patterns]] over time, like those in the popular "Gang of Four Design Patterns" book, a popular comp sci book that explains the theory of object-oriented design, but also offers standard patterns to use instead of having to design everything from first principles every time.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=51)** The equivalent design patterns book that addresses stability is Michael Nygard's "Release It!"
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=58)** It takes an application-first approach to understanding the causes of production failures, the patterns they take, and techniques to make applications resistant to those kinds of failures.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=69)** One point the book makes is that integration points are the number one cause of issues in an architecture, and what's known as the cascading failure of a service based on an issue with an integration point is the number one threat to stability in a layered architecture.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=86)** For example, if a database layer goes down or becomes slow, an application server tier fronting it can exhaust database connection pools and choke out the app server layer.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=98)** In modern architectures, especially in microservice architectures, apps end up having many integration points so the likelihood of one of them going bad is very high.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=109)** A stability pattern that can prevent cascading outages is called the Circuit Breaker, which watches for failures or slowness when calling an integration point.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=119)** And if it detects an unusual amount of failures, it stops making those calls instead of continuing to bombard a failing system.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=128)** Combined with timeouts, this technique can prevent the spread of an outage.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=133)** Once patterns like this are identified, you can make platform-level fixes for them.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=138)** Libraries like Resilience4j have been developed to provide these stability patterns in an easily consumable format for developers.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=147)** A good manifesto for how to produce service-ready software is called the twelve-factor app, described at [12factor.net](https://12factor.net).
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=156)** For example, the third factor, Config, states that runtime configuration that varies by deployment should be separated out from the app code, stored in environment variables, and be fully independent of each other and not bundled into environment groupings.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=172)** This avoids fragility and improves portability.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=175)** This site is definitely worth studying.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=178)** A good way to get a bite-sized introduction to architectural terms is by following Martin Fowler.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=184)** He's a well-known developer who succinctly describes everything from page objects to serverless to bimodal IT.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=192)** He has a lot of great thoughts on [[DevOps]] related topics from the perspective of a highly experienced software engineer.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=200)** That may seem like a lot of theory to start out with, but that's what design is.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=205)** Thoughtful planning of how your applications and the systems around it work.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-theory?u=76281980&t=210)** Take a minute to schedule some focus time on your calendar to look more deeply into these patterns and consider which may have value in your own particular technical ecosystem today based on the kinds of failures that you see in production.
