#### [Building for reliability: Practice](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=0)** - As I like to say, "Dev comes from school, but Ops comes from the street."
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=6)** Developers tend to come from computer science backgrounds, but a lot of system administrators are self-taught.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=12)** They learn from the real world of running large scale systems.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=16)** SRE is in large part bringing that hard one ops experience to the table and turning it from manual fiddling to disciplined engineering.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=25)** You are now about to witness the strength of street knowledge.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=29)** The hard truth is that all systems fail.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=33)** Codes often written with the assumption that failure of the underlying systems is if not impossible, at least very unusual and probably should result in some manual intervention.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=44)** But failure and slowdowns, which are as much of a threat as outages, are common.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=50)** In a modestly complex system individual components are failing all the time, as every operations engineer who's spent time looking through log files knows, and this doesn't always cause an outage.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=62)** System components are like slices of Swiss cheese stacked on top of each other.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=66)** If the holes in the slices don't line up, then things keep working.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=70)** But when they do, then users experience problems.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=75)** Medical Doctor Richard Cook wrote a key paper entitled, "How Complex Systems Fail", used first for resilience engineering and medicine, but it's been widely applied to tech as well.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=87)** Its key findings are echoed throughout our discussion of SRE.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=90)** Like, changes introduce new [[Forms]] of failure.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=94)** Complex systems contain changing mixes of failures latent within them.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=99)** All complex systems are always running in degraded mode.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=104)** In tech, we often refer to the number of nines of availability A system has, for example, a system with 99.9% uptime is said to have three nines of uptime, which is the equivalent of 8.77 hours of downtime a year.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=119)** A system with five nines is 99.999% available only 5.26 minutes of downtime per year.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=129)** For too long, every bit of it effort was put into preventing problems from happening in the first place.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=135)** The problem is this is incredibly difficult to do.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=139)** Pushing more and more money at highly available systems is a losing game.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=144)** I spent a lot of time in my career listening to excuses about why the system that, "doesn't have any single point of failure", still went down.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=154)** Instead, we approach our systems with the idea of resilience.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=159)** Resilience is the intrinsic ability of a system to maintain or regain a dynamically stable state, which allows it to continue operations after a major mishap and or in the presence of a continuous stress.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=174)** Resilience Engineering focuses on understanding how complex adaptive systems respond to a surprise or disruption.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=182)** So instead of trying to engineer a single perfect component, we use tools like; redundancy, running multiple identical copies of a server application container or the like, so that we can maintain service if we lose one.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=197)** We use load balancers or other traffic shaping devices to send user traffic to the healthy parts of the system.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=204)** Then from a performance perspective, we create automatic scaling, so that when there's too much load, we don't need to get a bigger server.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=212)** We can just add more servers or other components to grow to accommodate load.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=217)** And then from there you can automate further failover and recovery activity.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=222)** A [[Kubernetes]] installation, for example, runs redundant copies of both its core services and each of its applications across multiple servers, has health checking built in, performs automatic failover when one goes down, replicates the cluster state in multiple places, all kinds of coping mechanisms to keep its system up and responding in the face of single or even multiple component failures.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=249)** But there's no perfect mouse trap and that's not even your complete system.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=255)** People are part of your system.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=257)** The actions of people can break parts of your system and the actions of people can help maintain your system's health.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=265)** An important part of resilience engineering is realizing that all systems are sociotechnical.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=271)** In other words, they consist of the computers, but also the people who create, maintain and use them.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=277)** Don't spend so much time planning for the perfect system uptime that you fail to acknowledge, it's really going to be at least partially broken all the time.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=287)** And expert practitioners are needed to intervene.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=291)** SREs build applications to maintain and fix the systems they work on.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=296)** In fact, a common rubric is that SREs should be spending at least half of their time developing tools and not just fixing things manually.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=306)** Writing runbooks ways to safely intervene in system operations, better ways to feedback monitoring into controlling the system.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=315)** And remember ensuring your services running isn't just for operations engineers, developers are experts on their code and should be involved in resolving problems.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=326)** This means developers are on call to help when their code goes awry.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=330)** "You write it, you run it."
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=331)** Is a common saying.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=333)** At [[Google]], the dev team support their own services until they convince the SRE team that their service is stable and has enough monitoring and tooling to be supported by another team in production.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=346)** Developers should learn to use their debugger, profiler and application performance management tools in a runtime environment.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/building-for-reliability-practice?u=76281980&t=354)** You're a critical part of the resilience picture as well.

#### [Operational feedback: Observability](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=0)** - How do we know what state of failure our systems are in so we can take action if we need to?
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=6)** And beyond that, how do we use this information to feed back into development to improve our service using the three ways?
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=13)** Observability is a measure of how well the internal states of a system can be inferred from knowledge of its external outputs.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=20)** In other words, by looking at the metrics and logs, that we are monitoring on a given service, can we really tell what's going on?
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=28)** There are many ways to instrument your system to work towards it being observable, but let's take a look at the five areas of observability we suggest measuring: synthetic checks, system and application metrics, end-user performance, system and application logs, and finally, security.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=48)** You can test a running services performance and uptime by programmatically hitting it and checking the results.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=54)** These are often referred to as synthetic checks or health checks, synthetic, because they're not from real user traffic.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=61)** It's the simplest form of monitoring to answer the question of, is it working?
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=65)** Health checks are often run on sub-components as well, but a high level service health check is usually the first thing you need.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=73)** Try to hit my homepage and tell me if it's up.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=76)** The next set of things you can monitor are metrics that can be measured from your systems and applications.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=82)** System metrics are things like CPU or memory.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=85)** These are time series numerical data stored in Graft, where you can look at them and answer the question of, is this service, host, or process functioning normally?
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=95)** You can make your applications emit custom metrics, which can be a lot more diagnostic.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=100)** Looking at system or platform metrics and trying to guess what's really going wrong inside an application is a hassle.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=107)** A few examples include emitting how long a certain function call is taking, or the number of logins in the last hour, or error event counts.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=117)** Let's talk about [[Performance Metrics]].
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=120)** Tied through all the previous types of metrics are hints about your performance.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=124)** However, I want to call out real user monitoring and application performance monitoring, which give a much more accurate look at performance.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=133)** Application Performance Monitoring, or APM, is an instrumentation framework that reports performance at the code level.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=141)** It can tell you how long every function took to run, how long every API call took, and how long every database query took.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=148)** Real User Monitoring, also called RUM, usually uses front-end instrumentation, for example, a [[JavaScript]] page tag to capture activity and performance as observed by the users of a system.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=160)** It's able to tell you exactly what your customers are experiencing.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=164)** You can combine these ideas to perform tracing across a complex system and observe the path of a request as it spreads across many smaller services, and how long each piece of work took.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=176)** Logging is a great source of more detailed information than just, did it work?
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=181)** Or how long did it take?
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=182)** Because logs convey additional text, and because you can develop your application to write whatever logs you want, and you can tell details about what happened, when it happened, where did it happen, and what was involved.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=197)** You can use logs for problem detection, troubleshooting, audit and compliance, [[Capacity Planning]], security forensics, and much more.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=206)** The last area is security monitoring.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=209)** Security monitoring often uses existing logs and metrics, but augments them with ways to detect specific threats.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=216)** This can include looking for specific indicators of compromise, like certain paths on endpoints, connections from known bad IPs, bad configurations, or suspicious or unusual behavior on your network systems.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=230)** Examples include spikes in failed login activity, injection attempts on your website, or even malformed network requests.

> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=252)** You'll want to analyze what kinds of monitoring helps you support your systems in production, and what helps the development team improve the applications.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-observability?u=76281980&t=260)** Because monitoring isn't just for production performance and uptime, it's also a source of valuable information to developers about how the service is really used out in production.

>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/operational-feedback-incident-response-and-retrospectives?u=76281980&t=262)** So [[Building Trust]] via transparency builds goodwill.

#### [Your DevOps SRE toolchain](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are two parts to a site reliability engineering tool chain.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=4)** - The first part, building for reliability, is hard to give general guidance on.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=10)** It's going to be very different based on programming language and tech stack, and it's less about tools and more about libraries and development techniques
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=19)** - Right. The important thing to know is that there are books that describe these techniques, tools and libraries like [[Java]]'s Resilience4j, and dev and ops engineers need to collaborate at design time to determine what kind of built-in resilience approach to use.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=35)** - The second part to your tool chain, operational feedback, uses a fairly common set of observability and [[Incident Response]] tooling.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=44)** - Yeah, this is a very rich space with offerings for every kind of monitoring and suites to combine them.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=50)** - [Presenter in Red] You have [[SaaS]] offerings like Datadog, Honeycomb, and SumoLogic.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=56)** - You have open source tools like Nagios, Grafana, and Prometheus.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=60)** - And commercial software like Solarwinds and Splunk.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=64)** - In fact, of all the tooling areas in [[DevOps]], this is the one where there really might be too many options.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=71)** - Well, might be second after [[Kubernetes]].
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=74)** - Okay, that's accurate.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=75)** Anyway, not only are there a dizzying amount of tool options, but also, this is a part of your system that it's really the worst to design completely upfront.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=85)** - Why? While there are some commonalities, the specific service you build is going to have its own observability needs that you won't fully understand until you've seen it work in production.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=98)** - [James] Observability is a key place to use your lean techniques.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=103)** Our five areas of tooling are synthetic checks, system and application metrics, end-user performance, system and application logs, and security monitoring.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=114)** - Instead of guessing which ones we need to invest in, let's use our lean build measure learn approach when creating observability.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=123)** First, build. Create a minimum viable monitoring stack, synthetic monitors on a couple endpoints, [[System Monitoring]] to get basic stats, performance latency from logs, and so on.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=137)** Implement the quickest, cheapest option for all five areas.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=141)** - Okay, second. Measure.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=143)** You know, just start getting metrics from each of those areas of monitoring.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=147)** - And third, learn.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=148)** Analyze the application stack with the monitoring in place and then see where you need more to solve your problems.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=156)** Where are you needing more detailed system metrics?
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=159)** Can the app developers tell what's going on in their app from their own logs, or do they need to iterate and improve them?
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=165)** And then repeat this cycle and go deeper as needed.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=169)** - This lets you hone in on what kind of information really helps you support your service, because monitoring you don't use, that's waste.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=178)** I worked on one team where we were so vigorous about our monitoring that we realized a full 30% of our system load was coming from our own monitoring.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=187)** That was not so great.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=189)** - James and I were working together in one place turning a piece of packaged software into an online service.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=196)** The developer was new to the web world and his application logs were initially terrible, but that was okay, because it allowed us to learn.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=205)** The third way of DevOps at work.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=207)** - Yeah. As we worked towards making the service production ready and we'd have an issue, we'd pull him in and show him his own logs and say, hey, what's going on here?
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=216)** And he couldn't tell, but then he'd improve his logs.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=219)** Next we said, now, if you improve them so much that one of us knows what's going on, then we'd have to call you in here less.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=227)** And the logging grew strong with that one.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=230)** - Now we're measuring. But are we sharing?
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=234)** As you build out your observability stack, remember that an SRE or ops engineer is not the only person who can benefit from feedback from your production applications.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=244)** - Yeah. Developers can use error and performance information to further improve their app.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=249)** You know, product managers and business decision makers, they need to know how things are going, what to budget for, and so on.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=257)** - And go beyond their built-in reports and [[Dashboards]], and perform custom development where needed.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=264)** I was working at a SaaS provider that deeply integrated into many major retail websites.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=269)** We built a custom visualization that looked like the whiteboard picture that people across the business generally understood our service to look like and marked it up with error rates and performance that would turn, you know, green, yellow, or red based on status.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=285)** Every sales rep knew where their big customer was, for example, on cluster five, so it made the monitoring meaningful to them.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=294)** At a glance, people across the company could reassure themselves that things were fine.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=299)** And when clusters did begin to run hot, there was an immediate gathering of the right people to be involved in the incident.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=306)** - Okay. Now, speaking of incident response, there are also good tools for incident response and management, and runbook automation to use for, you know, like, routine activities.
>
> **[5:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=316)** Pagerduty, for example, is a SaaS solution that takes basic alerts from observability tools and allows for rich on-call scheduling and escalation workflows to allow for clean incident response.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=328)** There's others, like VictorOps and OpsGenie.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=332)** - Rundeck is a runbook automation tool that I've used to set up both emergency and routine maintenance activities.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=339)** There are others, like [[Ansible]] Tower and StackStorm.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=343)** I usually use Rundeck myself, because it has open source, commercial, and SaaS options.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=348)** - And finally, there are status page tools you can use to transparently communicate outages to users, like [[Atlassian]] Statuspage, [Status.io](https://Status.io), and some others.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=358)** - [Presenter in Red] There's a lot of SRE tooling, and while those are the primary areas, there are many more, and this is a place where organizations end up writing a lot of custom tooling to fit their specific use case.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/your-devops-sre-toolchain?u=76281980&t=370)** - Remember, the key is to keep it simple and keep your people in mind, and then select and iterate a tool chain that best lets your entire team collaborate around supporting your environment.

### 7. Advanced Topics

#### [Platform engineering: The paved road](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=1)** - So how the heck do you create your [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|infrastructure as code]], make your builds continuous, have great [[Incident Response]] and make it all secure and compliant while demanding developers and complaining end users are all up in your business?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=15)** You can stay on top of it for a little while, but how do you scale it out?
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=19)** Because if you align along value streams, as you create multiple streams and have many dev and ops teams creating solutions, you get a greater diversity of solutions which can lead to chaos.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=32)** Well, this is where the ah in comms comes to the rescue, automation.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=39)** The first organizations that hit extreme scale in [[DevOps]], like Netflix, Meta, [[Google]] and Spotify hit this and invested in creating self-service automation for people to get what they need out of the system.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=52)** This approach is referred to as creating a golden path or a paved road.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=57)** While a lot of the early discovery in DevOps had the flavor of blazing a trail through the wilderness.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=63)** Once you figure out the things you need, you can build something to make it better, faster, and easier to use, an opinionated framework that does these things in a certain way.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=75)** And then having the paved road incentivizes more people to use it.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=80)** Sure, your team could come up with their own CI/CD pipeline or observability set up, but that's pretty complicated to do, right?
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=89)** Unless you really do have specific needs, it's easier to use the one being provided for you and then improvements to that road benefit more people when you make them and team members find it easier to move from project to project.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=105)** CI/CD pipelines are one of the most basic examples of a paved road.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=109)** You have automated check-in hooks, run tests automatically on pull request, do automatic test deployments, all things that used to be manual and error prone, and now people have a common right way to do it that makes it faster and better.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=125)** I've worked on platforms that let end users spin up their own cloud account to develop in and their own HPC cluster to do machine learning on.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=134)** This ends up being a really, really good dynamic.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=138)** Say you build a solution that lets users provision their own cloud [[Hardware]] and run their own builds, easily plug into your standard observability and get security guidance as an automated service.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=151)** This prevents all the waste and delay of waiting on other teams to perform low value work for you, and you can build in guidance and compliance to the structure instead of leaving it to process and human memory.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=165)** Recently, highly comprehensive paved road creation has been rebranded as platform engineering.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=171)** Instead of a paved road, don't you want a supersonic train?
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=175)** Something that tightly combines development, test, deploy, infrastructure creation, observability, security, runtime environment, scaling, service discovery.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=188)** Platform engineering can be defined as the discipline of designing and building tool chains and workflows that enable self-service capabilities for software engineering organizations.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=199)** Many organizations are investing in building out internal platforms and some vendors are starting to sell 'em.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=206)** That can be a good idea, but it can also go wrong.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=210)** I'm going to cut to the chase and ask you directly, what's the difference between a cool modern DevOps platform and the horrid, frustrating centralized systems that many of us have been forced to waste our time with by distant uncaring IT departments over the decades, did we just spend a decade working to reinvent centralized IT?
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=234)** The difference is subtle, and that's the first challenge.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=238)** Many old school control focused IT orgs want to slap a fresh coat of paint on their old painful centralized systems, call it a platform and ship it.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=249)** Good platforms optimize the global system.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=253)** They don't optimize for some central team's need, they optimize the value streams.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=259)** The key ways to do this are to manage platforms like their product and take a lean approach.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=266)** First, product management.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=269)** Platforms need to serve their users, not their creators.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=272)** Remember that compliance and process are waste from a lean perspective.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=277)** Don't confuse them with the value.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=279)** You should optimize for value.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=282)** Real [[Microsoft Products|products]] that companies make for customers have product managers that help uncover their requirements, make sure the product is good, and work on marketing the product to the consumers.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=294)** Netflix and other platform centric organizations don't even make using their platforms mandatory.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=300)** They encourage their use, but just like in the real world, if somebody's forced to use your product, that product probably gets pretty bad over time.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=311)** Second, a lean approach.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=313)** There is absolutely such a thing as too much platform.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=317)** Platforms take a lot of investment, and if you build too much upfront, it's pretty likely that you don't actually know what your needs are and you're wasting that development time.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=328)** Blaze a trail, then pave the road, then build your train, learn where people need to go and how they need to get there before you invest in infrastructure.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=340)** Full end-to-end platforms can be great if they're laser focused on making your dev and ops workers job easy and they don't constrain innovation by adding overhead or preventing you from doing new things when that's the right thing to do.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/platform-engineering-the-paved-road?u=76281980&t=355)** Please share your own experiences in the course Q&A section with platforms that have worked well for you or have tormented you for community discussion.

> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=104)** The attempt to fix this is called [[DevSecOps]].

> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=117)** Well, [[DevOps]] is supposed to include everyone, but the term has turned out to be a useful bridge to help security folks feel welcome.

> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=126)** No matter what you call it, we're bringing DevOps principles and practices to security.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=131)** Let's revisit our DevOps culture, CAMS, culture, automation, measurement, and sharing with a security lens.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=139)** The culture of DevSecOps is best captured in this tenant created by Zane Lackey and Rich Smith over at Etsy.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=145)** It says, "If security introduces blocking to the organization, it will be ignored, not embraced."
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=152)** This is a reminder that security works alongside developers.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=155)** They can't slow down delivery by adding more gates and barriers to the work of delivering software.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=160)** Otherwise, the value stream will do what it does best with any blocker and it'll route around it.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=167)** Automation builds in security and security tools to the development and operational environment.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=173)** One way to do this is called shifting left.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=175)** Since value streams are often represented as flowing from the far left to the far right, shifting left means introducing security earlier in the development process and not waiting to do some kind of scan once everything is already done and in production.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=190)** This means using security tools earlier in the development lifecycle, often in the IDE and the CI system where developers are already working.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=198)** Because CI/CD theory tells us that the sooner we catch issues like using fast feedback, the easier it is to fix them.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=206)** Shifting tools left in the pipeline, it sounds like a no-brainer, but since I've started a company to make security easy for developers at Dry Run Security, I've heard many stories of how shift left got applied to the detriment of developers.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=220)** It's kind of classic platform engineering gone bad, where an InfoSec team just dumps their own work on all the developers optimizing for themselves, not for overall value creation.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=232)** This can present itself as bloated bill times or forcing developers to parse archaic security tools.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=239)** Shifting left can be a good thing, but it must be aligned to the value stream and designed so that security has minimal effect on the overall cycle time.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=247)** Sharing means building bridges by involving devs and ops in sharing information and responsibility both ways with the InfoSec team.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=256)** An under-resourced security team can deputize security champions in each group.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=261)** You find them by hosting Capture the Flag events, searching code repos for past security bug fixers, or just asking for volunteers.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=270)** This is a good way to jumpstart a culture shift where security becomes part of the delivery of the software.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=276)** The InfoSec team can train the champions and the champions help them understand the concerns, processes, and values of the product teams.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=284)** Measurement establishes good observability and metrics around security and uses them to create joint goals the teams can agree on.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=292)** In some places, security relies on FUD, you know, fear, uncertainty, and doubt, to just try and push teams into doing what they want the way they want.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=301)** But that's not very inspiring for engineers and it's not very DevOps or very DevSecOps.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=306)** Okay, DevSecOps is a big topic.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=308)** There are several courses on it in the library, including a few that I've written that would be worth exploring.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devsecops-making-your-systems-more-secure-the-devops-way?u=76281980&t=313)** No matter what you call it, whether DevSecOps or just DevOps, security is critical and there are newer, better ways to go about getting it together.

>

>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=47)** The first reason is that it's a platform framework that lets you automate a lot of the plumbing required to run your applications.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=54)** Besides the deployment and scaling, it lets you add in management features, like observability, service discovery, health monitoring, custom networking, and many others in a standardized way so that application developers get a lot of capabilities without having to build them themselves.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=72)** - Right, and it also abstracts the infrastructure away, allowing compute, networking, and storage to be managed by the Kubernetes platform.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=81)** This has resulted in Kubernetes becoming somewhat of a standard to deploy and manage your apps across on-premise, different cloud providers and so on.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=91)** Attempts at making applications multi-cloud were never very good.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=94)** But since Kubernetes add such a thick service layer, it mostly accomplishes this goal.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=100)** You just take a containerized application, say you want some number of redundant instances deployed across the multiple nodes in the cluster, expose an API and you're good to go.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=112)** - [Ernest] As a result, Kubernetes has become very popular.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=115)** It lets one team worry about building out the platform, and others worry about writing their apps to deploy on it.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=122)** - And since it got so popular, people have built many tools and [[Microsoft Products|products]] to add onto it.

>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=170)** - Kubernetes is good and popular, but like any technology, you have to be careful when and how you use it to get the benefits.

>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=195)** So most Kubernetes platforms end up consisting of at least a dozen stitch together tools.
>

>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=224)** - [James] Yeah, this is a good point.

>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=237)** Because when an incident happens, lack of understanding of what is going on under the hood, that will become a problem.

>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=288)** - And finally, it can work against your DevOps goals if you're not careful.

> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=292)** We already talked about platform engineering and how a platform can be a great force multiplier for developers, or it can just make things easier for the team that owns it, while making things harder for everyone else.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=306)** If you're not using systems thinking and keeping your DevOps CAMS values in mind as you move on to Kubernetes, you can easily generate silos and waste and not gain the benefits you were hoping for out of the considerable effort that it takes to do it.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/cloud-native-and-kubernetes?u=76281980&t=322)** - So Kubernetes is a good tool to learn about and a very popular tool.

#### [DevOps and chaos engineering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=0)** - Chaos.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=2)** That doesn't sound great when we're talking about our IT systems.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=6)** might be fun as an alignment when we're playing Dungeons and Dragons and want our characters to act up, but we want to eliminate chaos from our workplace, right?
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=14)** Well, there's an advanced topic in resilience called chaos engineering.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=20)** Chaos engineering is the discipline of experimenting on a system in order to build confidence in the system's capability to withstand real world conditions in production.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=30)** In other words, creating deliberate adversity for your system.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=35)** What does that even mean?
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=36)** Well, Netflix introduced the ideas we know it today with a tool that they called the Chaos Monkey.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=43)** Netflix was running huge clusters of cloud systems for millions of users.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=48)** They told their technical staff to build a system that was resilient in the face of problems.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=52)** It should keep on working if any particular server or network link or other component failed.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=58)** So, they'd write and test their code, including the operational code designed to make the whole system stay up and working with that in mind.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=66)** But they realized pretty quickly that the fault testing they were doing as part of their usual CICD process wasn't enough and that it couldn't be, they couldn't put together a test system consisting of all of the thousands of other components in the Netflix ecosystem and couldn't emulate the craziness that the real production system faced in contact with millions of internet users every day.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=90)** They were trying to make a resilience system but didn't have a way to validate that except for waiting for real production problems to happen, which is always a very upsetting time for everyone.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=102)** So they made the Chaos Monkey.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=105)** The Chaos Monkey is an angry monkey.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=107)** It goes into their production system and breaks something itself.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=112)** That's right, what better way to force every single tech team to make sure that the Netflix system won't be degraded when a server goes down than to have the Chaos Monkey go in and smash a server every once in a while?
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=124)** And it worked great.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=125)** It helped them continually improve the resilience of their system by creating failures and learning from them before failures inevitably showed up on their own.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=135)** And so, we have chaos engineering.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=137)** Now, it's controlled chaos.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=140)** It's not really as simple as shock the monkey.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=142)** In Chaos engineering, you construct experiments that you want to validate via some kind of fault injection.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=150)** You do fault testing in your development process, too, of course, this isn't an excuse to be sloppy.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=156)** Testing and production is cool, but not testing and development is just foolish.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=161)** If you believe a given fault should be automatically remediated in your environment, then chaos experiments can help validate that.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=170)** But it's also used for faults that require human intervention.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=175)** Activities called Game Days can be held where a significant fault is either emulated or even really created where the human part of [[Incident Response]] is tested as well.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=186)** Remember, all our systems are sociotechnical systems.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=189)** If we don't test our incident response, then we shouldn't be surprised when it's poor.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=195)** James and I worked at a startup building chaos experiments for [[Kubernetes]].
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=200)** Since it's a complex system, there are many different things that can go wrong with it.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=204)** And unless you try breaking those things, you don't know how your system will respond, and more importantly, what that looks like to a human operator.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=214)** IT systems aren't binary, at least not above the chip level.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=218)** What happens when you lose a server or just one application container on a server?
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=224)** Does it recover? How fast does it recover?
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=227)** What happens if that server actually reconnects later?
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=232)** We found that people's assumptions about how their systems would behave were not strongly correlated with how they actually did behave when something broke.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=242)** Break the network, break the container repository, break DNS.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=246)** It lets you get past guessing how that will look to experimenting so you really know.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=252)** It's like automobile crash tests.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=254)** They don't just build a car they think is going to be sturdy.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=257)** They break it a hundred times under different scenarios to figure out what happens when it does crash.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=265)** I like chaos engineering because it's an example of innovative thinking, breaking one of the walls of constraint that we labor under without even thinking about it and saying, "But what could we learn and improve if we did this in production instead?"
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=280)** Chaos Engineering is all about becoming a learning organization, part of our target [[DevOps]] culture, and it's a direct application of the three ways of DevOps.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/devops-foundations-23454205/devops-and-chaos-engineering?u=76281980&t=289)** Use feedback loops to create learning.
